import torch
import reference
import solution

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
shape = {'batch': 4, 'num_heads': 8, 'num_kv_heads': 2, 'head_dim': 128, 'seq_len': 256, 'page_size': 16}
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
    setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)

init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()

torch.manual_seed(42)
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]

with torch.no_grad():
    ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
    sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)

print('any nan in sol:', torch.isnan(sol_out).any().item())
print('any nan in ref:', torch.isnan(ref_out).any().item())
print('any inf in sol:', torch.isinf(sol_out).any().item())
print('any inf in ref:', torch.isinf(ref_out).any().item())
max_abs = (ref_out - sol_out).abs().max().item()
print('max_abs:', max_abs)

import reference
import solution

OK here's the simpler kernel for Option A: ```python @triton.jit def _attn_kernel( Q_ptr, KV_ptr, BT_ptr, SL_ptr, O_partial_ptr, LSE_partial_ptr, sm_scale, stride_qb, stride_qh, stride_kvb, stride_kvp, stride_kvh, stride_btb, stride_btblock, stride_op_b, stride_op_h, stride_op_s, stride_op_d, stride_lse_b, stride_lse_h, stride_lse_s, BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, # = G BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, P: tl.constexpr, SPLIT_K: tl.constexpr, CHUNK: tl.constexpr, ): bid = tl.program_id(0) hkv = tl.program_id(1) split = tl.program_id(2) seq_len = tl.load(SL_ptr + bid) start = split * CHUNK end = tl.minimum(start + CHUNK, seq_len) h_start = hkv * BLOCK_M offs_m = h_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M) offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) offs_d_v = D + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) q_ptrs = Q_ptr + bid * stride_qb + offs_m[:, None] * stride_qh + offs_d[None, :] q = tl.load(q_ptrs) # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) m_i = tl.full([BLOCK_M], -float("inf"), dtype=tl.float32) l_i = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M], dtype=tl.float32) acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32) if start < end: first_page = start // P last_page = (end - 1) // P for p in range(first_page, last_page + 1): block_id = tl.load(BT_ptr + bid * stride_btb + p * stride_btblock) tok_idx = p * P + offs_n valid = (tok_idx >= start) & (tok_idx < end) k_addr = (KV_ptr + block_id * stride_kvb + offs_n[:, None] * stride_kvp + hkv * stride_kvh + offs_d[None, :]) k = tl.load(k_addr, mask=valid[:, None], other=0.0) v_addr = (KV_ptr + block_id * stride_kvb + offs_n[:, None] * stride_kvp + hkv * stride_kvh + offs_d_v[None, :]) v = tl.load(v_addr, mask=valid[:, None], other=0.0) s = tl.dot(q, tl.trans(k)) # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N) s = s * sm_scale s = tl.where(valid[None, :], s, -float("inf")) m_new = tl.maximum(m_i, tl.max(s, axis=1)) alpha = tl.exp(m_i - m_new) p = tl.exp(s - m_new[:, None]) l_i = l_i * alpha + tl.sum(p, axis=1) acc = acc * alpha[:, None] + tl.dot(p.to(tl.bfloat16), v).to(tl.float32) m_i = m_new l_i_safe = tl.where(l_i > 0, l_i, 1.0) o_partial = (acc / l_i_safe[:, None]).to(tl.bfloat16) lse = m_i + tl.log(l_i_safe) else: o_partial = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.bfloat16) lse = tl.full([BLOCK_M], -1.0e30, dtype=tl.float32) o_ptrs = (O_partial_ptr + bid * stride_op_b + offs_m[:, None] * stride_op_h + split * stride_op_s + offs_d[None, :] * stride_op_d) tl.store(o_ptrs, o_partial) lse_ptrs = (LSE_partial_ptr + bid * stride_lse_b + offs_m * stride_lse_h + split * stride_lse_s) tl.store(lse_ptrs, lse) ``` BLOCK_M = G = 4 or 8. If I use BLOCK_M=16, BLOCK_KV=1 (and accept that 12 of the 16 query heads use wrong K), the compute is 16*16*128 = 32K ops, but it's WRONG (4x less compute but also wrong). So the choice between BLOCK_M=4 (with 12 dummies padded) and BLOCK_M=16 (with 12 dummies that use wrong K) is: - BLOCK_M=4: correct but 32K ops per program per page. 4 programs per batch per kv head. Total: 4 * num_pages * 32K. - BLOCK_M=16 (wrong): 32K ops per program per page. 1 program per batch per kv head. Total: 1 * num_pages * 32K. So BLOCK_M=4 has 4x more total compute but 4x more parallelism. For memory-bound, both should perform similarly. Let me go with BLOCK_M=4 (the GQA group size). This is correct and natural. Wait, but Triton might not support M=4. Let me check. Actually, looking at Triton 3.6 source, I believe tl.dot supports M >= 16 for tensor cores. Smaller M might require a different code path (no tensor cores). Let me just try and see. If it fails, I'll switch to BLOCK_M=16 with the 3D batched approach. Actually, I think I should just try BLOCK_M=16 with the correct K loading (3D batched). This is the safest. The 4x compute overhead is acceptable. OK let me rewrite the kernel with 3D batched. ```python @triton.jit def _attn_kernel( Q_ptr, KV_ptr, BT_ptr, SL_ptr, O_partial_ptr, LSE_partial_ptr, sm_scale, stride_qb, stride_qh, stride_kvb, stride_kvp, stride_kvh, stride_btb, stride_btblock, stride_op_b, stride_op_h, stride_op_s, stride_op_d, stride_lse_b, stride_lse_h, stride_lse_s, BLOCK_KV: tl.constexpr, # number of kv heads per program G: tl.constexpr, # group size BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, # = BLOCK_KV * G BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, P: tl.constexpr, SPLIT_K: tl.constexpr, CHUNK: tl.constexpr, ): bid = tl.program_id(0) hkv_blk = tl.program_id(1) split = tl.program_id(2) seq_len = tl.load(SL_ptr + bid) start = split * CHUNK end = tl.minimum(start + CHUNK, seq_len) hkv_start = hkv_blk * BLOCK_KV h_start = hkv_start * G offs_m = h_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M) offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) offs_d_v = D + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) offs_kv = hkv_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_KV) # Load Q: (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) -> reshape to (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) q_ptrs = Q_ptr + bid * stride_qb + offs_m[:, None] * stride_qh + offs_d[None, :] q = tl.load(q_ptrs) q3 = tl.reshape(q, (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D)) m_i = tl.full([BLOCK_M], -float("inf"), dtype=tl.float32) l_i = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M], dtype=tl.float32) acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32) if start < end: first_page = start // P last_page = (end - 1) // P for p in range(first_page, last_page + 1): block_id = tl.load(BT_ptr + bid * stride_btb + p * stride_btblock) tok_idx = p * P + offs_n valid = (tok_idx >= start) & (tok_idx < end) # Load K: (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) k_addr = (KV_ptr + block_id * stride_kvb + offs_n[:, None, None] * stride_kvp + offs_kv[None, :, None] * stride_kvh + offs_d[None, None, :]) k = tl.load(k_addr, mask=valid[:, None, None], other=0.0) # Load V: (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) v_addr = (KV_ptr + block_id * stride_kvb + offs_n[:, None, None] * stride_kvp + offs_kv[None, :, None] * stride_kvh + offs_d_v[None, None, :]) v = tl.load(v_addr, mask=valid[:, None, None], other=0.0) # Permute K to (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D) k3 = tl.permute(k, (1, 0, 2)) # (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D) # QK^T batched: (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_N) s3 = tl.dot(q3, tl.permute(k3, (0, 2, 1))) # (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_N) s3 = s3 * sm_scale s3 = tl.where(valid[None, None, :], s3, -float("inf")) # Flatten for softmax s = tl.reshape(s3, (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N)) m_new = tl.maximum(m_i, tl.max(s, axis=1)) alpha = tl.exp(m_i - m_new) p = tl.exp(s - m_new[:, None]) l_i = l_i * alpha + tl.sum(p, axis=1) # Reshape p for matmul: (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_N) p3 = tl.reshape(p, (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_N)) # Permute V to (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D) v3 = tl.permute(v, (1, 0, 2)) # (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D) # PV batched: (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) o3 = tl.dot(p3.to(tl.bfloat16), v3) # (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) o2 = tl.reshape(o3, (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D)).to(tl.float32) acc = acc * alpha[:, None] + o2 m_i = m_new l_i_safe = tl.where(l_i > 0, l_i, 1.0) o_partial = (acc / l_i_safe[:, None]).to(tl.bfloat16) lse = m_i + tl.log(l_i_safe) else: o_partial = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.bfloat16) lse = tl.full([BLOCK_M], -1.0e30, dtype=tl.float32) o_ptrs = (O_partial_ptr + bid * stride_op_b + offs_m[:, None] * stride_op_h + split * stride_op_s + offs_d[None, :] * stride_op_d) tl.store(o_ptrs, o_partial) lse_ptrs = (LSE_partial_ptr + bid * stride_lse_b + offs_m * stride_lse_h + split * stride_lse_s) tl.store(lse_ptrs, lse) ``` Hmm, this is more complex. Let me also think about whether tl.permute and tl.reshape work as I expect. For tl.permute with axes (1, 0, 2) on a tensor of shape (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D): new shape is (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D). Original index [n, kv, d] -> new index [kv, n, d]. Correct. For tl.dot with 3D inputs, the batch dim is the first. The matmul is: out[b, m, n] = sum_k a[b, m, k] * b[b, k, n]. So we need a of shape (B, M, K) and b of shape (B, K, N). For Q @ K^T: q3 is (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) and we want K^T of shape (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_N). k3 is (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D). So we need to permute k3 by (0, 2, 1) to get (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_N). Then tl.dot(q3, k3_perm) = (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_N). Yes, this is what I have. For tl.reshape: Triton supports reshape between compatible shapes. (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) -> (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) should work if BLOCK_M = BLOCK_KV * G. Let me check. For BLOCK_KV=4, G=4, BLOCK_M=16. Actually, you know what, let me also consider an alternative. Use a 2D matmul but interleave the K heads in a clever way. For BLOCK_KV=4, the K data for one page and 4 kv heads is 16 tokens * 4 kv heads * 128 = 16 * 4 * 128 = 8192 elements. If I lay it out as (16, 4, 128), the K for (token, kv) is at position [token, kv, :]. For the matmul, I want to compute Q @ K^T where Q is (16, 128) and K is (16*4, 128) = (64, 128). But the K rows for query head m are K[16 * (m//G) : 16 * (m//G) + 16, :]. So head 0 uses K[0:16], head 1 uses K[0:16], head 2 uses K[0:16], head 3 uses K[0:16]. Heads 4..7 use K[16:32], etc. To do this with a flat matmul, I'd need to "scatter" the K rows. Specifically, for head m, the K row is K[16 * (m//G) + n, :] for token n. This is a strided access pattern. tl.dot doesn't support this directly. OK 3D batched it is. Let me code. Actually, I realize I should also consider: maybe the 3D batched matmul internally uses more efficient code (since the M=4 padding is the same regardless of batch, the compiler can amortize). Or maybe Triton has special handling for small M. Let me just try. Let me write the kernel. Wait, actually I realized there's an even simpler approach. For G=4, we can flatten the (BLOCK_KV, G) into a single M dim of size BLOCK_KV*G = 16. But for the matmul, we want each group of 4 query heads to use a different K head. What if we lay out Q such that query heads 0..3 are stored at positions 0..3, query heads 4..7 at positions 4..7, etc.? That's the natural layout. But for the matmul, we want to use K head 0 for positions 0..3, K head 1 for positions 4..7, etc. If K is laid out as (BLOCK_N * BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) where the first BLOCK_N rows are K head 0, next BLOCK_N are K head 1, etc., then for query head m, the K rows are K[BLOCK_N * (m // G) : BLOCK_N * (m // G) + BLOCK_N, :]. This is a strided gather. tl.dot doesn't support this directly. UNLESS we transpose K to (BLOCK_D, BLOCK_N * BLOCK_KV) where the first BLOCK_N columns are K head 0, etc. Then the matmul is Q @ K with Q (16, 128) and K (128, 64) = (16, 64). The columns for head m are K[:, BLOCK_N * (m // G) : BLOCK_N * (m // G) + BLOCK_N]. We need to gather these to the right positions in the output. This is essentially a "block-diagonal" matmul. The output is (16, 64) where output[m, n] = Q[m, :] @ K[:, BLOCK_N * (m//G) + n]. The output is "block-diagonal" in the sense that for fixed m, the relevant columns are at specific positions. We can compute this as a flat matmul (16, 64) and then "unblock" the result. The unblock is: for m, output[m, n] is at position m, BLOCK_N * (m//G) + n. This is essentially a strided store. But this requires interleaving the output writes, which is awkward in Triton. OK let me just go with 3D batched. It's the cleanest. Actually, hmm, wait. import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
        setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    with torch.no_grad():
        ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
        sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)
    
    has_nan = torch.isnan(sol_out).any().item()
    has_inf = torch.isinf(sol_out).any().item()
    if has_nan or has_inf:
        print(f'Shape {shape}: NaN={has_nan} Inf={has_inf}')
    else:
        max_abs = (ref_out - sol_out).abs().max().item()
        ok = max_abs < 0.02
        print(f'Shape {shape}: max_abs={max_abs:.4e} ok={ok}')

uv run python -c "
# Analyze per-shape parallelism
import math
shapes = [
    {'B': 8, 'H': 32, 'Hkv': 8, 'D': 128, 'L': 1024, 'G': 4, 'name': 'shape 0'},
    {'B': 32, 'H': 32, 'Hkv': 8, 'D': 128, 'L': 2048, 'G': 4, 'name': 'shape 1'},
    {'B': 4, 'H': 64, 'Hkv': 8, 'D': 128, 'L': 4096, 'G': 8, 'name': 'shape 2'},
    {'B': 16, 'H': 32, 'Hkv': 8, 'D': 128, 'L': 1535, 'G': 4, 'name': 'shape 3'},
    {'B': 8, 'H': 16, 'Hkv': 4, 'D': 64, 'L': 2000, 'G': 4, 'name': 'shape 4'},
]

for chunk in [64, 128, 256, 512]:
    print(f'CHUNK={chunk}:')
    for s in shapes:
        BLOCK_KV = max(1, -(-16 // s['G']))
        BLOCK_KV = min(BLOCK_KV, s['Hkv'])
        BLOCK_M = BLOCK_KV * s['G']
        HKV_BLKS = s['Hkv'] // BLOCK_KV
        SPLIT_K = max(1, 1 << (s['L'] // chunk - 1).bit_length() if s['L'] // chunk > 0 else 0)
        SPLIT_K = max(1, -(-s['L'] // chunk))
        # next pow2
        sk = (s['L'] + chunk - 1) // chunk
        SPLIT_K = max(1, 1 << (sk - 1).bit_length() if sk > 0 else 0)
        total = s['B'] * HKV_BLKS * SPLIT_K
        print(f'  {s[\"name\"]}: BLOCK_M={BLOCK_M} BLOCK_KV={BLOCK_KV} HKV_BLKS={HKV_BLKS} SPLIT_K={SPLIT_K} -> total={total} programs (vs 188 SMs = {total/188:.2f} waves)')
" 2>&1 | tail -40

Let me write this. Actually, I realize there's another approach. What if I use a 2D matmul but expand the M dim to cover multiple kv heads? Like BLOCK_M = BLOCK_KV * G with BLOCK_KV=1 and G=4, so BLOCK_M=4. Then I pad BLOCK_M to 16 (with dummies). The matmul is (16, 128) @ (128, 16) = (16, 16). 12 dummies. But the dummies would have wrong K (since BLOCK_KV=1, all dummies use the same K as the valid 4 heads). So this is wrong. To use BLOCK_M=16 with BLOCK_KV=1 correctly, I'd need to load K for all 16 query heads' kv heads, which is BLOCK_KV=4 (since G=4 and 16/4=4 kv heads). That's what I had before. OK let me just try BLOCK_M=G (BLOCK_KV=1) and see. Hmm wait, actually let me think about another angle. What if I use BLOCK_M=16 and BLOCK_KV=4 (as before) but improve the memory access pattern? The K load is (BLOCK_N=16, BLOCK_KV=4, BLOCK_D=128). In memory, this is 16*4*128 = 8192 bf16 = 16KB. The access pattern is: for each (n, kv), we read 128 contiguous bf16 values. The K for (n, kv) is at offset KV + block_id * stride_kvb + n * stride_kvp + (hkv_start + kv) * stride_kvh + d * stride_kvd. For coalesced access, we want consecutive threads to read consecutive memory addresses. The default Triton layout for tl.load is row-major in the last dim. So for our load, threads should access d in [0, 128) for the same (n, kv). That's contiguous, so coalesced. Across (n, kv), the access is less contiguous. For different kv, we have stride_kvh * 2 bytes = 2*D*2 = 512 bytes apart. For different n, stride_kvp * 2 bytes = Hkv * 2 * D * 2 = Hkv * 4 * D bytes. For Hkv=8, D=128, that's 4096 bytes. So consecutive n are 4KB apart. The memory access pattern: 128 contiguous bytes (for one n, one kv, all d), then 4KB to the next n (same kv), then 512 bytes to the next kv (same n). Hmm, the kv dimension is interleaved within the n dimension. Actually, in memory, the layout is (n_outer, kv_inner, d). So for one n, we have all kv's d. Then the next n. So we read 4*128*2 = 1KB per n, then jump 4KB to the next n. For coalesced access, the 32 threads in a warp should read 32 contiguous bytes. With 4 kv heads and 128 d, that's 1024 bytes per n, which is 32 transactions (32 bytes each). 8 warps could handle this in parallel. With 4 warps, 16 transactions per warp. Should be fine. Hmm I think the access pattern is OK. Let me focus on parallelism. OK let me just try CHUNK=128 and see if it helps. Also let me try num_warps=8. Actually, I think the bottleneck for shape 0 might be the kernel launch overhead and the per-program work being too small. With 0.34 waves, most SMs are idle in the second wave, but the first wave might be quick. Let me just run experiments. Let me also consider: what if I make the K and V loads use vectorized loads (e.g., load 4 bf16 at a time = 8 bytes)? Triton handles this automatically based on alignment. Let me check the access pattern. The K data is (BLOCK_N=16, BLOCK_D=128) bf16. The inner dim is 128 * 2 = 256 bytes. With bf16, each thread can load 8 bytes (4 elements). 256/8 = 32 elements per thread. With 32 threads in a warp, we can load 32*8 = 256 bytes per cycle. That's one row of 128 bf16. For BLOCK_N=16 rows, we need 16 row-loads. With multiple warps, this can be parallelized. OK I think the access pattern is fine. Let me just try smaller CHUNK. Actually, I realized there's something else I should consider. The reduction kernel might be slow. For shape 0 with B=8, H=32, the reduction has 8*32=256 programs. For each program, it loads SPLIT_K=4 LSEs and 4 partial outputs (each 128 elements). So it reads 4*4 + 4*128*2 = 1032 bytes per program. Total: 256*1032 = 264KB. That's tiny, fast. The reduction should not be the bottleneck. OK let me try smaller CHUNK. CHUNK=128 means SPLIT_K=8 for shape 0 and 16 for shape 4. Let me also try CHUNK=64 for max parallelism. Actually, let me also consider: with smaller CHUNK, the reduction kernel has more partials to combine. For SPLIT_K=16, the reduction reads 16*4 + 16*128*2 = 4144 bytes per program. For shape 4 with B=8, H=16, that's 8*16*4144 = 530KB. Still tiny. Let me try CHUNK=128 first.

uv run python -c "
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
        setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    with torch.no_grad():
        ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
        sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)
    
    has_nan = torch.isnan(sol_out).any().item()
    has_inf = torch.isinf(sol_out).any().item()
    if has_nan or has_inf:
        print(f'Shape {shape}: NaN={has_nan} Inf={has_inf}')
    else:
        max_abs = (ref_out - sol_out).abs().max().item()
        ok = max_abs < 0.02
        print(f'Shape {shape}: max_abs={max_abs:.4e} ok={ok}')
" 2>&1 | tail -10

Now let me think about other optimizations. Let me look at the kernel and see where time is being spent. Ideas: 1. Load K and V together (one 2*D load instead of two D loads). This might improve memory coalescing. 2. Use vectorized loads (Triton should do this automatically). 3. Pre-compute the K^T to avoid the permute in the matmul. 4. Use shared memory more efficiently. Let me look at the current kernel structure: ```python for p in range(first_page, last_page + 1): block_id = tl.load(BT_ptr + ...) # 1 int load k = tl.load(KV_ptr + ..., mask=valid[:, None, None], other=0.0) # (16, 4, 128) bf16 v = tl.load(KV_ptr + ..., mask=valid[:, None, None], other=0.0) # (16, 4, 128) bf16 k3 = tl.permute(k, (1, 0, 2)) kt3 = tl.permute(k3, (0, 2, 1)) s3 = tl.dot(q3, kt3) s3 = s3 * sm_scale s3 = tl.where(valid[None, None, :], s3, -float("inf")) s = tl.reshape(s3, (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N)) m_new = tl.maximum(m_i, tl.max(s, axis=1)) alpha = tl.exp(m_i - m_new) p = tl.exp(s - m_new[:, None]) l_i = l_i * alpha + tl.sum(p, axis=1) p3 = tl.reshape(p, (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_N)) v3 = tl.permute(v, (1, 0, 2)) o3 = tl.dot(p3.to(tl.bfloat16), v3) o2 = tl.reshape(o3, (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D)) acc = acc * alpha[:, None] + o2 m_i = m_new ``` Per page: - 1 block_id load - 2 K/V loads (16*4*128 = 8KB each = 16KB) - 1 matmul (16x16x128 = 32K ops effective, 128K with padding) - Some permutes and reshapes - 1 matmul (16x16x128 = 32K ops) - Online softmax For shape 0 (64 pages per program, 128 programs, 8 batches): - Total K/V loaded: 8 * 1024 * 8 * 128 * 4 = 4MB K + 4MB V = 8MB. The L2 cache (128MB) can hold the entire KV cache, so subsequent reads might be cached. Wait, no. Each program reads its own subset of the KV cache. Actually, let me try one more optimization: use a single K/V load instead of two separate loads. The K and V are interleaved in memory as (..., 2*D). A single load of (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, 2*D) and then split into K and V. ```python # Load KV combined: (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, 2*BLOCK_D) kv_addr = ( KV_ptr + block_id * stride_kvb + offs_n[:, None, None] * stride_kvp + offs_kv[None, :, None] * stride_kvh + tl.arange(0, 2 * BLOCK_D)[None, None, :] ) kv = tl.load(kv_addr, mask=valid[:, None, None], other=0.0) # Split into K and V k = kv[:, :, :BLOCK_D] # ... not sure how to slice in Triton v = kv[:, :, BLOCK_D:] ``` Triton has `tl.split` for splitting. Or we can just load them separately. Hmm, splitting in Triton might not be free. Let me think. Actually, the issue with loading K and V together is that the access pattern is 2*D contiguous, and we'd be reading 2*16*4*128 = 16KB worth of data (vs 16KB in two separate loads). Same total. The benefit would be if the compiler can issue fewer load instructions. For bf16, 2*D=256 elements per token = 512 bytes. Each thread can load 16 bytes (8 bf16). So 32 threads (1 warp) can load 512 bytes (one row). With 4 kv heads and 16 tokens, we have 64 rows = 64 warp-loads. With 4 warps, 16 loads per warp. That should be fine. OK the access pattern is probably fine. Let me look at the kernel structure more. Actually, I realize: for BLOCK_M=16, BLOCK_N=16, the matmul is small. Tensor cores on Blackwell do 16x16x16 = 4K ops per warp instruction. For (16, 128) @ (128, 16), we have 128/16 = 8 K-dim chunks. So 8 tensor core ops per warp. With 4 warps (128 threads), 2 ops per warp. That's 2 instructions per warp. Pretty small. Maybe a larger BLOCK_N would help. Let me try BLOCK_N=32 (2 pages per iteration). The matmul would be (16, 128) @ (128, 32), with 2x more K-dim and 2x more N-dim. Total compute is 2x. With BLOCK_N=32 and same K, the matmul is 16*32*128 = 64K ops. With M=4 padded to 16, 4 batches * 16*32*128 = 256K ops per page. 2x more than BLOCK_N=16. For 32 pages instead of 64, 32 * 256K = 8M ops per program. For 128 programs, 1G ops. Same as before. Just less loop overhead. Hmm, loop overhead is small. The main benefit of larger BLOCK_N is reducing the per-iteration overhead (block_id load, valid compute, etc.). Per iteration overhead: 1 block_id load + 2 K/V loads + 2 matmuls + softmax. The K/V loads dominate (16KB). The other ops are O(1) per iteration. For BLOCK_N=32, we have 2 pages per iteration, so 32 iterations. Overhead is 32 * O(1) + 32 * 16KB = 512KB. Wait that's wrong, the K/V load is 2x larger per iter (32 tokens * 4 kv * 128 D = 32KB), so total K/V per program is 32KB * 32 iters = 1MB. Same as before. So no benefit from BLOCK_N=32 in terms of total memory. The benefit would be reducing per-iter overhead. But the overhead is small. OK let me look at other optimizations. Actually, let me think about: with BLOCK_M=16, the matmul (16, 128) @ (128, 16) might not be using tensor cores efficiently. The M=16 is exactly the warp size, so each warp does one row of the output. The N=16 is also one warp. So 16x16 per warp = 256 outputs per warp. With 4 warps (for the M=16 dim), each warp does 64 outputs. Hmm. Wait, actually for tensor cores, the warp does a 16x16x16 matmul. So for (16, 128) @ (128, 16), the warp does 8 ops (K=128, K-chunks=8). Each op gives 16x16 outputs. So 8 * 256 = 2048 outputs per warp. With 16x16 = 256 outputs total, we need 1 warp. So 1 warp can do the whole matmul. With 4 warps, 3 warps are idle. That's wasteful. To use more warps, we'd need a larger M or N. Like BLOCK_M=32, BLOCK_N=32 would need 4 warps. For shape 0 with B=8, I could have 1 program per (b, hkv_blk, split), and 1 program per (b+1, hkv_blk, split), etc. So 2 batches per program. Then 64 programs instead of 128. But each program does 2x the work. For shape 0, B=8 batches, BLOCK_B=2, then 4 batch-blocks per "row". With HKV_BLKS=2 and SPLIT_K=8, total = 4*2*8 = 64 programs. Half of 128. Worse. OK that doesn't help. Let me think about num_warps more. For BLOCK_M=16, BLOCK_N=16, BLOCK_D=128, the matmul is small. num_warps=2 might be enough. num_warps=4 (current) might be overkill. Let me try num_warps=2. Actually, I realized I should also think about the K load. The K load is (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) = (16, 4, 128) bf16 = 8KB. With 4 warps, each warp loads 2KB = 1024 bf16 = 64 bf16 per thread. That's 128 bytes per thread, or 32 bytes per transaction. With 32 threads per warp, 32 * 32 = 1024 bytes per warp transaction. 2KB / 1024 = 2 transactions per warp. 4 warps = 8 transactions. That's fast. For V, same. 8 + 8 = 16 transactions per page. With 64 pages, 1024 transactions per program. With 128 programs, 131K transactions. Hmm, that's a lot. Actually, the issue is the layout. For (16, 4, 128), threads need to coalesce. The default Triton layout should be fine. OK let me just try a few things and benchmark. Let me try: 1. num_warps=2 2. BLOCK_M=32 (process 8 kv heads per program for shape 0/1/3/4, 4 for shape 2) 3. Per-shape tuning Actually, I realize I haven't thought about pre-computing the K^T layout. The permute in the matmul is `tl.permute(k3, (0, 2, 1))`. This is a layout change, not a data movement. So it's free in terms of memory. But the dot product might be more or less efficient with different layouts. Let me not worry about this for now. Let me also think about: the softmax has `tl.exp` and `tl.max` operations. These are element-wise. For BLOCK_M=16, the softmax is 16*16 = 256 elements per page. Tiny. The matmul is the main compute. Let me also consider: maybe I should use a multi-page K/V load (load 2 pages at a time) and do 2 matmuls or a 2x larger matmul. Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me just try the simple BLOCK_N=32 approach. Wait, BLOCK_N must be a multiple of P (page_size) for the page boundary tracking. Or we can have BLOCK_N=2*P=32 with internal page boundary handling. For BLOCK_N=32, the K data spans 2 pages (or 1 page boundary). The block_table needs 2 entries. The mask needs to handle the boundary. Let me write this carefully. ```python # BLOCK_N = 2 * P offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) # 0..31 # p_in_block = offs_n // P # 0 or 1 # p_offset = offs_n % P # 0..15 # In each iter, process BLOCK_N tokens for p in range(first_page, last_page_in_chunk + 1): # The chunk covers [start, end) tokens # We process tokens in groups of BLOCK_N # The page indices for the BLOCK_N tokens p_idx_in_chunk = (start // BLOCK_N) + (p - first_page) // 2 # approximate ... ``` This is getting complex. Let me think. Actually, for BLOCK_N=32, the loop iterates over (start, start+32), (start+32, start+64), etc. Each iteration loads 2 pages. The block_table reads 2 page indices per iter. But the chunk size is still 256 (or whatever). Each split has CHUNK=256 tokens = 8 BLOCK_N=32 iterations. Let me think about the simplest way to do this. Each iteration: 1. If start=0, tokens 0..31 are in pages 0 and 1. If start=8, tokens 8..31 are in page 0 (tokens 0..7 are before start) and tokens 32..39 are in page 1. Wait, that doesn't work because we're loading 32 tokens at a time. Let me think again. For BLOCK_N=32, we load 32 tokens at a time. The starting token index in the seq is some value t. The 32 tokens are t, t+1, ..., t+31. These span at most 2 pages (if t is at a page boundary, they span 1 page; if t is in the middle of a page, they span 2 pages). The page index for token i is i // P. For P=16, the page index changes at multiples of 16. If t=0, tokens 0..15 are in page 0, tokens 16..31 are in page 1. If t=8, tokens 8..15 are in page 0, tokens 16..31 are in page 1. If t=16, tokens 16..31 are in page 1. So the pages for 32 consecutive tokens are (t//16, t//16 + 1) or (t//16, t//16) if t is page-aligned. For loading, I need to load K for both pages and stitch them together. The K for the first page has 16 tokens, the second has 16 tokens. I want a contiguous 32 tokens. Let me think of an alternative. For BLOCK_N=32, I can have 2 separate K loads, one for each page, and concatenate. But the matmul needs a single (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D) tensor. Alternative: load 2 pages separately, and use 2 separate matmuls (or batched). But then BLOCK_N is effectively 16 per matmul. Then BLOCK_N=16 (one page) and the loop is over pages. We have BLOCK_N=16, which is what we have now. No change. Hmm. So increasing BLOCK_N requires more complex page handling. For shape 0 (B=8) with 4 batches per program: B/BLOCK_B=2, total = 2*2*8 = 32 programs. Worse. OK that's not better. Let me think about pre-computing. If I compute the partial output and LSE in one kernel, then the reduction is fast. The main bottleneck is the first kernel. Let me also think about the L2 cache. The KV cache for one batch is small enough to fit in L2. For shape 0, per batch: 1024 * 8 * 256 = 256KB. L2 is 128MB, so many batches fit. But with 128 programs reading 1MB each, the total L2 traffic is 128MB. That's more than the 8MB total KV data. So L2 reuse is happening. Hmm, but L2 traffic is not the same as DRAM traffic. The DRAM traffic should be just the 8MB (4MB K + 4MB V) once. The L2 is just caching. OK so we're DRAM-bandwidth bound. We're getting 758 GB/s, which is 42% of 1.8 TB/s. So we're 20x slower than peak. Hmm wait, that's not right. The kernel time includes both reading and computing. If memory is the bottleneck, we'd take 4MB / 1.8TB = 2.2us. If compute is the bottleneck, we'd take 1G ops / 1650 TFLOPS = 0.6us. So memory is 4x slower than compute. We're at 44us, which is 20x slower than memory peak. That's bad. Why? Let me think. For shape 0, we have 128 programs running on 188 SMs. So only 0.68 waves. The first wave has 128 programs, the second has 0 (or 60 idle SMs). In the first wave, 128 programs run in parallel. No wait, the subsets don't overlap: each program reads K/V for a specific (batch, kv_head) range. For shape 0, B=8, HKV=8, BLOCK_KV=4. No, the splits partition the sequence, so each (batch, kv_head) is read by exactly 1 split per kv head block. Hmm wait, the splits are over the sequence. For 1 batch, 4 kv heads, 1024 tokens: 1024 * 4 * 128 * 4 = 2MB. 8 splits each read 1/8 = 256KB. Total 2MB. Good, no duplication. 8 batches * 2MB = 16MB. import torch
import reference
import solution
import sys

shape_idx = int(sys.argv[1]) if len(sys.argv) > 1 else 0
import shapes
shape = shapes.SHAPES[shape_idx]
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
    setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)

device = torch.device('cuda:0')

Triton supports `tl.broadcast_to` for shape expansion. Let me try this. Actually, in Triton, the typical way to do this is: ```python # Load K for BLOCK_KV heads: (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) k = tl.load(...) # shape (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) # Expand to (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) then to (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) = (G * BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) k = k[None, :, :] # (1, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) k = tl.broadcast_to(k, (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D)) # (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) k = tl.reshape(k, (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D)) # (G * BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) = (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) ``` Wait, broadcast_to from (1, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) to (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) replicates the first dim. The reshape of (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) to (G*BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) is: the first G elements of the new shape correspond to the first element of G (i.e., k[0, :, :]). So the new[0:G, :] = old[0, :, :]. Then new[G:2G, :] = old[1, :, :], etc. So new[m, :] = old[m // G, m % G, :]. But old is broadcast_to'd from (1, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) to (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D), so old[g, kv, :] = k_kv[kv, :]. Triton's reshape is row-major by default. For (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) reshaped to (G * BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D), the row-major order is: - new[0, :] = old[0, 0, :] - new[1, :] = old[0, 1, :] - ... - new[BLOCK_KV-1, :] = old[0, BLOCK_KV-1, :] - new[BLOCK_KV, :] = old[1, 0, :] - ... Wait, that's 4 query heads per group, all using the same K_kv. So we have 4 groups, each using the same K_kv[0..3]? That doesn't make sense. For GQA with G=4, query heads 0..3 use kv head 0, heads 4..7 use kv head 1, etc. So heads 0..3 should use k_kv[0], heads 4..7 should use k_kv[1], etc. Hmm. Let me re-examine. Bad. new[3, :] = old[0, 3, :] = k_kv[3, :]. Bad. new[4, :] = old[1, 0, :] = k_kv[0, :]. Good. So the layout is wrong! I need a different arrangement. I want: new[0] = k_kv[0], new[1] = k_kv[0], new[2] = k_kv[0], new[3] = k_kv[0], new[4] = k_kv[1], new[5] = k_kv[1], ... This is the transpose of what broadcast_to gives me. Alternative: use (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) instead of (G, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D). Then reshape to (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D): - old[kv, g, d] = k_kv[kv, d] (broadcast in g) - new[m, :] = old[m // G, m % G, :] For BLOCK_KV=4, G=4: - m=0: new[0, :] = old[0, 0, :] = k_kv[0, :] - m=1: new[1, :] = old[0, 1, :] = k_kv[0, :] - m=2: new[2, :] = old[0, 2, :] = k_kv[0, :] - m=3: new[3, :] = old[0, 3, :] = k_kv[0, :] - m=4: new[4, :] = old[1, 0, :] = k_kv[1, :] - ... So: ```python # Load K for BLOCK_KV heads: (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) k_kv = tl.load(...) # (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) # Expand to (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) k_expanded = k_kv[:, None, :] # (BLOCK_KV, 1, BLOCK_D) k_expanded = tl.broadcast_to(k_expanded, (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D)) # (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) # Reshape to (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) k = tl.reshape(k_expanded, (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D)) # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) ``` This gives k[m, :] = k_kv[m // G, :]. Correct! Then the matmul is (BLOCK_M=16, BLOCK_D=128) @ (BLOCK_D=128, BLOCK_N=16) = (16, 16). M=16, N=16, K=128. Uses tensor cores. But wait, the K data is now (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) with each row duplicated G times. The matmul is 4x more work than the minimum (since we compute the same K * Q for G query heads). Hmm. Actually, the matmul is (16, 128) @ (128, 16) = (16, 16). The Q is (16, 128). For rows 0..3 (group 0), Q is different per row, but K is the same. So S[0..3, n] = Q[0..3, :] @ K[0, :]. This computes the correct score for each of the 4 query heads (since Q is different per head). The matmul compute is 16*16*128 = 32K ops. With M=16 (real, no padding), this is the minimum. No waste. For the original 3D batched approach with M=4 padded to 16, the compute was 4 * 16*16*128 = 128K ops. 4x more. So the new approach (2D matmul with K broadcast) is 4x less compute. So I can just use the full P (with dummies) for the PV matmul. No need to extract and re-pad. Let me re-structure: 1. QK matmul: (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D) @ (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_N) = (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N). M=G_PADDED=16. 2. Softmax over the last dim. Output: (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N). 3. PV matmul: (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N) @ (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D) = (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D). M=G_PADDED=16. 4. Reshape to (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D). 5. Update acc for all BLOCK_M_PADDED rows (dummies' acc is not used). 6. Store with mask (only real rows). This is cleaner. Let me code it. OK here's the new kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _paged_attn_splitk_kernel( Q_ptr, KV_ptr, BT_ptr, SL_ptr, O_partial_ptr, LSE_partial_ptr, sm_scale, stride_qb, stride_qh, stride_kvb, stride_kvp, stride_kvh, stride_btb, stride_btblock, stride_op_b, stride_op_h, stride_op_s, stride_op_d, stride_lse_b, stride_lse_h, stride_lse_s, BLOCK_KV: tl.constexpr, G: tl.constexpr, G_PADDED: tl.constexpr, # max(G, 16) for tensor cores BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, # = BLOCK_KV * G (real heads per program) BLOCK_M_PADDED: tl.constexpr, # = BLOCK_KV * G_PADDED BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, P: tl.constexpr, SPLIT_K: tl.constexpr, CHUNK: tl.constexpr, ): bid = tl.program_id(0) hkv_blk = tl.program_id(1) split = tl.program_id(2) seq_len = tl.load(SL_ptr + bid) start = split * CHUNK end = tl.minimum(start + CHUNK, seq_len) hkv_start = hkv_blk * BLOCK_KV h_start = hkv_start * G offs_m = h_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M) offs_m_padded = hkv_start * G_PADDED + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M_PADDED) m_valid = (tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M_PADDED) % G_PADDED) < G offs_n = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) offs_d_v = D + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) offs_kv = hkv_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_KV) # Load Q (real heads only): (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) q_ptrs = Q_ptr + bid * stride_qb + offs_m[:, None] * stride_qh + offs_d[None, :] q = tl.load(q_ptrs) # (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) bf16 # Reshape to (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) and pad G to G_PADDED q_3d = tl.reshape(q, (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D)) zeros_q = tl.zeros((BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED - G, BLOCK_D), dtype=q.dtype) q_3d_padded = tl.cat(q_3d, zeros_q, dim=1) # (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D) m_i = tl.full([BLOCK_M_PADDED], -float("inf"), dtype=tl.float32) l_i = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M_PADDED], dtype=tl.float32) acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.float32) if start < end: first_page = start // P last_page = (end - 1) // P for p in range(first_page, last_page + 1): block_id = tl.load(BT_ptr + bid * stride_btb + p * stride_btblock) tok_idx = p * P + offs_n valid = (tok_idx >= start) & (tok_idx < end) # Load K: (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) k_addr = ( KV_ptr + block_id * stride_kvb + offs_n[:, None, None] * stride_kvp + offs_kv[None, :, None] * stride_kvh + offs_d[None, None, :] ) k = tl.load(k_addr, mask=valid[:, None, None], other=0.0) # Load V: (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) v_addr = ( KV_ptr + block_id * stride_kvb + offs_n[:, None, None] * stride_kvp + offs_kv[None, :, None] * stride_kvh + offs_d_v[None, None, :] ) v = tl.load(v_addr, mask=valid[:, None, None], other=0.0) # Permute K, V to (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_D) k3 = tl.permute(k, (1, 0, 2)) v3 = tl.permute(v, (1, 0, 2)) # QK^T batched: (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N) s3 = tl.dot(q_3d_padded, tl.permute(k3, (0, 2, 1))) s3 = s3 * sm_scale s3 = tl.where(valid[None, None, :], s3, -float("inf")) # Flatten to (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_N) for the softmax s = tl.reshape(s3, (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_N)) m_new = tl.maximum(m_i, tl.max(s, axis=1)) alpha = tl.exp(m_i - m_new) p = tl.exp(s - m_new[:, None]) l_i = l_i * alpha + tl.sum(p, axis=1) # Reshape p to (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N) for the PV matmul p3 = tl.reshape(p, (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N)) # PV batched: (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D) o3 = tl.dot(p3.to(tl.bfloat16), v3) o2 = tl.reshape(o3, (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D)) acc = acc * alpha[:, None] + o2 m_i = m_new l_i_safe = tl.where(l_i > 0, l_i, 1.0) o_partial = (acc / l_i_safe[:, None]).to(tl.bfloat16) lse = m_i + tl.log(l_i_safe) else: o_partial = tl.zeros([BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D], dtype=tl.bfloat16) lse = tl.full([BLOCK_M_PADDED], -1.0e30, dtype=tl.float32) # Store: only the real rows (m_valid mask) o_ptrs = ( O_partial_ptr + bid * stride_op_b + offs_m_padded[:, None] * stride_op_h + split * stride_op_s + offs_d[None, :] * stride_op_d ) tl.store(o_ptrs, o_partial, mask=m_valid[:, None]) lse_ptrs = ( LSE_partial_ptr + bid * stride_lse_b + offs_m_padded * stride_lse_h + split * stride_lse_s ) tl.store(lse_ptrs, lse, mask=m_valid) ``` This should use tensor cores. import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
        setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    with torch.no_grad():
        ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
        sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)
    
    has_nan = torch.isnan(sol_out).any().item()
    has_inf = torch.isinf(sol_out).any().item()
    if has_nan or has_inf:
        print(f'Shape {shape}: NaN={has_nan} Inf={has_inf}')
    else:
        max_abs = (ref_out - sol_out).abs().max().item()
        ok = max_abs < 0.02
        print(f'Shape {shape}: max_abs={max_abs:.4e} ok={ok}')

reasoning (7227 chars)
Triton's `tl.zeros` requires power-of-2 shapes. G_PADDED - G = 12, which is not a power of 2. I need to use a different approach to pad. Options: 1. Use `tl.full` with the same constraint (power of 2). 2. Use `tl.cat` differently. 3. Pad to the next power of 2 (16), but the extra rows are also dummies. For option 3, I can pad G from 4 to 16, and the padding is 12 (not power of 2). So I can't use `tl.zeros` with shape (BLOCK_KV, 12, BLOCK_D). Let me use a different padding approach. I can pad to 16 by loading Q with extra rows (dummies) at offs_m beyond the real range. Specifically, load Q as (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) where BLOCK_M_PADDED = BLOCK_KV * 16. The first BLOCK_M = BLOCK_KV * G rows are real, the next BLOCK_M_PADDED - BLOCK_M are dummies (loaded as 0 with mask). But as I analyzed earlier, the reshape (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) -> (BLOCK_KV, 16, BLOCK_D) gives a layout where the first batch has all real Q and the other batches have all dummies. Not what I want. Hmm. Let me think. Actually wait, the reshape is (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) -> (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D). In row-major, the first G_PADDED rows become batch 0, the next G_PADDED rows become batch 1, etc. If I load Q as (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) with the first BLOCK_M real and the rest dummies, then: - Batch 0: first G_PADDED = 16 rows. The first G=4 are real (Q[0..3]), the next 12 are dummies (0). - Batch 1: next 16 rows. All dummies (since we only have G=4 real per batch, and batch 1 should have Q[4..7]). So the layout is wrong for batches 1+. To get the correct layout, I need to interleave the real Q across batches. Specifically: - Batch 0, rows 0..3: Q[0..3] - Batch 1, rows 0..3: Q[4..7] - Batch 2, rows 0..3: Q[8..11] - Batch 3, rows 0..3: Q[12..15] This is a strided gather. Not a simple contiguous load. In Triton, I can do this by: 1. Loading Q as (BLOCK_M, BLOCK_D) = (16, 128). 2. Reshaping to (BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_D) = (4, 4, 128). 3. Padding the G dim from 4 to 16. For step 3, I need to create a (4, 16, 128) tensor. The first 4 rows of each batch are Q_3d, the next 12 are 0. I can do this with `tl.where` and broadcasting: - Q_3d is (4, 4, 128). - Broadcast to (4, 16, 128) by adding a dim: Q_3d[:, None, :, :] is (4, 1, 4, 128). Broadcast to (4, 16, 4, 128). - Then use a mask to take only the first 4 along the new dim. Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me think. Actually, the simplest way: use a 4D tensor and then reshape. Or, just have the Q data laid out correctly from the start. Load Q as (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) where the layout matches the (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D) reshape. The layout for (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D) -> (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) is: - new[0, :] = old[0, 0, :] (batch 0, g=0) - new[1, :] = old[0, 1, :] (batch 0, g=1) - ... - new[15, :] = old[0, 15, :] (batch 0, g=15) - new[16, :] = old[1, 0, :] (batch 1, g=0) - ... For the real Q (batch kv, g in [0, G)): - new[kv*16 + g, :] = Q[hkv_start*G + kv*G + g, :] = Q[h_start + kv*G + g, :] So the layout is: new[m, :] = Q[h_start + (m // 16) * G + (m % 16), :] for m % 16 < G, else 0. To load this layout, I need to gather Q at the right offsets. The offsets are: - For m in [0, BLOCK_M_PADDED): offs = h_start + (m // 16) * G + (m % 16) if (m % 16) < G, else skip. In Triton, I can compute these offsets and use `tl.load` with a mask. Let me try this approach. ```python padded_offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M_PADDED) # 0..BLOCK_M_PADDED-1 # Compute the real Q offset for each padded row batch_idx = padded_offs // G_PADDED g_within = padded_offs % G_PADDED is_real = g_within < G real_offs = h_start + batch_idx * G + g_within # (BLOCK_M_PADDED,) # Load with mask q_ptrs = Q_ptr + bid * stride_qb + real_offs[:, None] * stride_qh + offs_d[None, :] q_padded = tl.load(q_ptrs, mask=is_real[:, None], other=0.0) # (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) # Reshape to (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D) q_3d_padded = tl.reshape(q_padded, (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D)) ``` This gives the correct layout! Let me try this. For the store, I need to map back from padded to real: - real_offs = h_start + (padded_offs // 16) * G + (padded_offs % 16) - m_valid = (padded_offs % 16) < G ```python real_offs = hkv_start * G + (padded_offs // G_PADDED) * G + (padded_offs % G_PADDED) m_valid = (padded_offs % G_PADDED) < G o_ptrs = O_partial_ptr + bid * stride_op_b + real_offs[:, None] * stride_op_h + ... tl.store(o_ptrs, o_partial, mask=m_valid[:, None]) ``` OK let me update the kernel. Wait, the `real_offs` formula is the same for load and store. Let me just compute it once. Also, I realize I don't need to reshape for the matmul. I can use the 2D layout (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) directly. The matmul: Q @ K^T where Q is (BLOCK_M_PADDED, BLOCK_D) and K is (BLOCK_D, BLOCK_N). For GQA, the K for query head m is K_data[n, m // 16 * (G_PADDED // G) + (m // G) ... hmm this is getting confusing. Wait, in the padded layout, the "query head" is m in [0, BLOCK_M_PADDED). The corresponding real query head is h_start + (m // 16) * G + (m % 16) [if m % 16 < G]. The KV head for this query head is (h_start + (m // 16) * G + (m % 16)) // G = hkv_start + (m // 16) + (m % 16) // G = hkv_start + m // 16 (since m % 16 < G implies (m % 16) // G = 0). So the KV head for padded query m is hkv_start + m // 16. The K for padded query m is K_data[n, hkv_start + m // 16, :]. For the matmul Q @ K^T: - S[m, n] = sum_d Q[m, d] * K[n, d] - We want S[m, n] = sum_d Q[m, d] * K_data[n, hkv_start + m // 16, d] So K[n, d] should be K_data[n, hkv_start + m // 16, d], which depends on m. For a 2D matmul, K is the same for all m. So we can only compute S for one m at a time. UNLESS we use the 3D batched matmul. The batch dim is m // 16 = batch_idx. The K for each batch is K_data[n, hkv_start + batch_idx, :]. So the 3D batched matmul is (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D) @ (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_N) = (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N). M=G_PADDED=16, uses tensor cores. To get the right Q layout, I need Q_3d[kv, g, d] = Q_padded[kv * 16 + g, d] = Q[h_start + kv * G + g, d] if g < G, else 0. This is what I had before. The issue was creating the padded Q with `tl.zeros` requiring power-of-2 shape. Let me use the new approach: load Q with the right offsets and mask. ```python # Compute the real Q offset for each padded row padded_offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M_PADDED) batch_idx = padded_offs // G_PADDED g_within = padded_offs % G_PADDED is_real = g_within < G real_offs_for_q = h_start + batch_idx * G + g_within q_ptrs = Q_ptr + bid * stride_qb + real_offs_for_q[:, None] * stride_qh + offs_d[None, :] q_padded = tl.load(q_ptrs, mask=is_real[:, None], other=0.0) q_3d_padded = tl.reshape(q_padded, (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D)) ``` This should work. The dummies are loaded as 0 (via mask), and the real Q is at the right offsets. For the matmul: q_3d_padded @ k_3d^T = (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_N). M=16, tensor cores. For the PV matmul: p_3d @ v_3d = (BLOCK_KV, G_PADDED, BLOCK_D). The p_3d is the full padded softmax output (including dummies). M=16, tensor cores. For the store: real_offs = hkv_start * G + (padded_offs // G_PADDED) * G + (padded_offs % G_PADDED). Mask: (padded_offs % G_PADDED) < G. Let me update the kernel.

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
        setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    with torch.no_grad():
        ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
        sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)
    
    has_nan = torch.isnan(sol_out).any().item()
    has_inf = torch.isinf(sol_out).any().item()
    if has_nan or has_inf:
        print(f'Shape {shape}: NaN={has_nan} Inf={has_inf}')
    else:
        max_abs = (ref_out - sol_out).abs().max().item()
        ok = max_abs < 0.02
        print(f'Shape {shape}: max_abs={max_abs:.4e} ok={ok}')

uv run python -c "
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
        setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    with torch.no_grad():
        ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
        sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)
    
    has_nan = torch.isnan(sol_out).any().item()
    has_inf = torch.isinf(sol_out).any().item()
    if has_nan or has_inf:
        print(f'Shape {shape}: NaN={has_nan} Inf={has_inf}')
    else:
        max_abs = (ref_out - sol_out).abs().max().item()
        ok = max_abs < 0.02
        print(f'Shape {shape}: max_abs={max_abs:.4e} ok={ok}')

uv run python -c "
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape in shapes.SHAPES:
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k.upper(), v)
        setattr(solution, k.upper(), v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    with torch.no_grad():
        ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)
        sol_out = sol_model(*inputs)
    
    has_nan = torch.isnan(sol_out).any().item()
    has_inf = torch.isinf(sol_out).any().item()
    if has_nan or has_inf:
        print(f'Shape {shape}: NaN={has_nan} Inf={has_inf}')
    else:
        max_abs = (ref_out - sol_out).abs().max().item()
        ok = max_abs < 0.02
        print(f'Shape {shape}: max_abs={max_abs:.4e} ok={ok}')
" 2>&1 | tail -10

1	"""Paged attention decode via Triton.
2	
3	Strategy: Flash-Decoding (split-K) with online softmax. Each program processes
4	one (batch, kv-head-block, sequence-chunk) and writes a partial output + LSE.
5	A second kernel reduces the partials to the final output.
6	
7	GQA grouping: process BLOCK_KV consecutive KV heads per program. The Q tile
8	has BLOCK_M = BLOCK_KV * G query heads (covering all G queries per kv head).
9	K is loaded as (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) and the matmul is 3D-batched so
10	each (kv-head, group) pair sees the right K.
11	"""
12	from __future__ import annotations
13	
14	import math
15	
16	import torch
17	import triton
18	import triton.language as tl
19	
20	
21	# Module-level knobs (overridden by check.py / benchmark.py from shapes.py).
22	BATCH = 8
23	NUM_HEADS = 32
24	NUM_KV_HEADS = 8
25	HEAD_DIM = 128
26	SEQ_LEN = 1024
27	PAGE_SIZE = 16
28	
29	
30	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
31	# Kernels
32	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
33	
34	
35	@triton.jit
36	def _paged_attn_splitk_kernel(
37	    Q_ptr, KV_ptr, BT_ptr, SL_ptr,
38	    O_partial_ptr, LSE_partial_ptr,
39	    sm_scale,
40	    # Q strides
41	    stride_qb, stride_qh,
42	    # KV strides

45	    stride_btb, stride_btblock,
46	    # O_partial strides

48	    # LSE_partial strides

1	"""Paged attention decode via Triton.
2	
3	Strategy: Flash-Decoding (split-K) with online softmax. Each program processes
4	one (batch, kv-head-block, sequence-chunk) and writes a partial output + LSE.
5	A second kernel reduces the partials to the final output.
6	
7	GQA grouping: process BLOCK_KV consecutive KV heads per program. The Q tile
8	has BLOCK_M = BLOCK_KV * G query heads (covering all G queries per kv head).
9	K is loaded as (BLOCK_N, BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D) and the matmul is 3D-batched so
10	each (kv-head, group) pair sees the right K.
11	"""
12	from __future__ import annotations
13	
14	import math
15	
16	import torch
17	import triton
18	import triton.language as tl
19	
20

22	BATCH = 8
23	NUM_HEADS = 32
24	NUM_KV_HEADS = 8
25	HEAD_DIM = 128
26	SEQ_LEN = 1024
27	PAGE_SIZE = 16
28	
29	
30	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
31	# Kernels
32	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
33	
34	
35	@triton.jit
36	def _paged_attn_splitk_kernel(
37	    Q_ptr, KV_ptr, BT_ptr, SL_ptr,
38	    O_partial_ptr, LSE_partial_ptr,
39	    sm_scale,
40	    # Q strides
41	    stride_qb, stride_qh,
42	    # KV strides

44	    # Block-table strides
45	    stride_btb, stride_btblock,

assistant
The solution is complete. Summary: **Final result**: `peak_fraction: 0.51` (geomean), with `PASS` from check.py. **Key design choices**: 1. **Flash-Decoding (split-K)**: Splits the sequence into chunks of 128 tokens, processed by separate programs. A second reduction kernel combines partial outputs using LSE. 2. **GQA via 3D batched matmul**: Each program processes `BLOCK_KV` consecutive KV heads (so `BLOCK_M = BLOCK_KV * G` query heads share K loads). The 3D batched matmul `(BLOCK_KV, G, BLOCK_N) @ (BLOCK_KV, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_N)` ensures each GQA group sees its own K head. 3. **Online softmax**: Standard FlashAttention accumulation with `m_i`, `l_i`, `acc` per query head. 4. **Per-shape BLOCK_KV**: Small-batch shapes use `BLOCK_KV=2` (G=4) or `BLOCK_KV=1` (G=8) to spawn more programs; large shapes use the full `BLOCK_KV=4` (G=4) or `BLOCK_KV=2` (G=8) to maximize tensor-core efficiency. 5. **CHUNK=128** (8 pages per chunk) balances per-program work vs split-K overhead. 6. **num_warps=2, num_stages=3** as a robust default. **Per-shape performance**: - Shape 0 (B=8, H=32, L=1024): 784 GB/s (44% of 1.8 TB/s peak) - Shape 1 (B=32, L=2048): 1337 GB/s (74%) - Shape 2 (B=4, H=64, L=4096): 1056 GB/s (59%) - Shape 3 (B=16, L=1535): 1143 GB/s (63%) - Shape 4 (B=8, H=16, D=64, L=2000): 506 GB/s (28%) The remaining gap to peak on shapes 0 and 4 is the small-batch parallelism; the 3D batched matmul with M=G=4 falls back to FMA (ncu confirms no tensor-core activity for M<16), which is the throughput bottleneck.
